Analysis of manufacturing AI transformation (AX) trends reveals that the core of successful AX is summarized as 'data' and 'strategy'.
The most prominent trend is the importance of building a robust data infrastructure based on OT-IT data integration and governance. This has emerged as a prerequisite determining the success or failure of AI adoption. Key application areas such as predictive maintenance, AI vision-based quality inspection, and production planning optimization are gaining attention based on high return on investment (ROI).
As an opportunity factor, next-generation technologies like 'Agentic AI', which autonomously perform tasks, are emerging as a new paradigm for manufacturing operations automation. On the other hand, difficulties in building data infrastructure, securing leadership support, and strengthening security in OT environments are pointed out as common challenges.
Therefore, for successful AX, securing executive support through clear ROI setting, along with the standardization of data pipelines including MLOps/DataOps and the establishment of a security system, are essential.

This article points out that the key reasons preventing pilot-to-production expansion are not 'technical problems,' but rather overlay-type transformations, lack of leadership accountability, inadequate data and process foundations, and cultural resistance. For application in manufacturing sites, it is necessary to clarify responsibilities, budgets, and schedules linked to KPIs; establish site/OT-centric data governance, standard schemas, and MDM; and from the outset, apply 'design for operationalization' including MLOps, OT-IT integration, and model monitoring/rollback, along with stage-gates. Furthermore, lowering resistance through site-participatory design and education (data literacy, citizen data scientists, CoE), and embedding AI into operational models such as predictive maintenance and dynamic optimization, then linking it with the compensation system, is a practical roadmap for successful expansion.

This article points out that AI adoption in manufacturing is hindered more by 'organization, data, and culture' issues than by 'technology', and presents success factors such as leadership accountability, data infrastructure establishment, managing on-site resistance, and operationalization-centric design (MLOps/OT-IT integration). It emphasizes that KPI linkage, stage-gate operation, standardization and governance, and education/competency internalization are essential for scaling pilots to operations. Practitioners should prioritize investment in improving data quality and accessibility, and change management, while management must redesign AI as a core identity of their business and operating models.

In the manufacturing AX environment, GenAI/LLM and OT/IT convergence assets must be separately identified and threat-modeled, and AI security verification must be strengthened, including input validation, prompt injection prevention, session, and API key misuse control. To prepare for APTs, internet-exposed Known Exploited Vulnerabilities (KEV), and phishing, prioritize applying a multi-layered defense that includes ISA/IEC 62443-based segmentation, remote access MFA, least-privilege IAM, and behavior-based EDR/XDR and monitoring, as well as external scanning and risk-based patching. As short-term actions, reduce downtime risk by blocking LLM/chatbot access to production networks, implementing proxy filtering and logging, immediately patching high-risk KEVs, and adopting administrator account vaulting and Just-In-Time (JIT) access.

This paper presents an adaptive testing framework that closed-loop integrates CFD and wind tunnel tests with AI-in-the-loop to recommend the next experimental conditions using online learning and reinforcement learning, demonstrating a 35% reduction in test iterations and a 12â€“15% improvement in performance (L/D). We propose an adaptive optimization method that directly deploys AI in UAV wind tunnel tests, integrating CFD-experiment-reinforcement learning into a closed loop. This approach reduces test costs and lead times and provides a practical AX framework that rapidly accelerates product/process performance.

Humanoid robots are rapidly improving in cost-performance due to the convergence of IT, AI, and robotics, and can become a mid-term automation alternative for human-like operational tasks such as material handling, final assembly assistance, inspection, and intralogistics within brownfield factories. Manufacturers should introduce them incrementally via pilot â†’ limited scale â†’ large-scale rollout, evaluate them using KPIs such as TCO, utilization rate, MTBF/MTTR, and defect impact, and must control cost reduction and vendor lock-in risk through standardization of common parts, data pipelines/synthetic data, open APIs, and multi-vendor interoperability. ViTrox expands inspection automation across all SMT/THT processes with AI-based 3D AOI, deep learning code readers, robot vision, and more. The core values are: (1) Line standardization and remote optimization through AI judgment (VVTS) and a central control tower, (2) Reduction of handling and cycle time risks with dual-sided 3D AOI, (3) Establishment of point quality gates for space-constrained processes with mini AOI/code readers, and (4) Complementing blind spots in conformal coating and final assembly inspection with robot vision. When applied, the AX effect can be maximized by combining KPI design focused on False Call, Escape Rate, labor costs, and OEE with data/model governance.

To meet the AIÂ·HPC demand in manufacturing sites, 10MW-class edge and 40MW-class hyperscale standard designs should be utilized as a capacity and expansion roadmap. Cooling decisions should be made from a TCO perspective, assuming a transition between air cooling â†” water cooling and liquid cooling (air cooling â†’ rear door heat exchanger â†’ D2C), while reflecting climate, electricity unit price, water resources, and site constraints. By integrating IT/OT and combining waste heat utilization with peak management, a smart factory's edge data center can meet latency and security requirements while achieving seamless expansion and ESG performance.

To align with the proliferation of generative AI and HPC, it proposes standard mechanical equipment designs for 40MW hyperscale and 10MW edge data centers, and emphasizes cooling strategies (liquid-cooled vs. air-cooled) that consider climate, site, and operating costs, a liquid cooling adoption roadmap, and an integrated design-construction-operation system based on commissioning. As distributed inference for applications such as vision inspection, digital twins, and AMRs expands within factories, 'AI-native networks' have become critical, and the integration of Private 5G/5G-A â€“ ensuring uplink-centric bandwidth, ultra-low latency, and stable QoS â€“ with MEC is essential infrastructure. ì´ ê¸€ì€ â€˜ë„¤íŠ¸ì›Œí¬ê°€ AIë¥¼ ê°€ëŠ¥í•˜ê²Œ í•œë‹¤â€™ëŠ” ê´€ì ì—ì„œ, ì œì¡° í˜„ì¥ì˜ AI(ë¹„ì „ ê²€ì‚¬, ë””ì§€í„¸ íŠ¸ìœˆ, ë¡œë´‡, AR ì •ë¹„ ë“±)ê°€ ì—£ì§€â€“í´ë¼ìš°ë“œ ë¶„ì‚° ì¶”ë¡ ê³¼ í•¨ê»˜ ì—…ë§í¬Â·ì €ì§€ì—°Â·ì•ˆì •ì  QoSë¥¼ ìš”êµ¬í•˜ê²Œ ë¨ì„ ê°•ì¡°í•©ë‹ˆë‹¤. 2031ë…„ AI íŠ¸ë˜í”½ì´ ë¹„AI íŠ¸ë˜í”½ì„ ë„˜ì–´ì„¤ ê²ƒì´ë©°, ì—£ì§€ í–¥ AI íŠ¸ë˜í”½ì´ ë¹ ë¥´ê²Œ ì„±ì¥í•  ê²ƒìœ¼ë¡œ ì „ë§í•©ë‹ˆë‹¤. ì œì¡°ì—…ì²´ëŠ” 5G-A ê¸°ë°˜ ì—…ë§í¬ ê°•í™”, ìŠ¬ë¼ì´ì‹±, MEC ì—°ê³„ë¥¼ ê³ ë ¤í•œ í”„ë¼ì´ë¹— 5G/5G-A ë¡œë“œë§µì„ ìˆ˜ë¦½í•˜ê³ , AI ì›Œí¬ë¡œë“œâ€“ë„¤íŠ¸ì›Œí¬ë¥¼ ê³µë™ ì„¤ê³„í•˜ì—¬ ë³‘ëª©ì„ ì œê±°í•´ì•¼ í•©ë‹ˆë‹¤. ìš°ì„ ìˆœìœ„ëŠ” (1) ê³µì •ë³„ ì—…ë§í¬/ì§€ì—° KPI ì •ì˜, (2) MEC ê·¼ì ‘ ë°°ì¹˜ë¡œ ì›ì²œë°ì´í„° êµ­ì§€ ì²˜ë¦¬, (3) SLA ê¸°ë°˜ ë„¤íŠ¸ì›Œí¬ ìŠ¬ë¼ì´ì‹± ë„ì…, (4) í´ë¼ìš°ë“œÂ·ì—£ì§€Â·ì˜¨ë””ë°”ì´ìŠ¤ ì¶”ë¡  ë¶„ë‹´ ìµœì í™”ì…ë‹ˆë‹¤. ìŠ¤í°ì„œ ì—°êµ¬ë¡œ ì„¸ë¶€ êµ¬í˜„ì§€í‘œëŠ” ë¶€ì¡±í•˜ì§€ë§Œ, ì „ëµì  ë°©í–¥ì„±ê³¼ íŠ¸ë˜í”½ ì „í™˜ ì‹œì‚¬ì ì€ ì œì¡° AXì— ìœ ì˜ë¯¸í•©ë‹ˆë‹¤. This article, from the perspective that 'networks enable AI,' emphasizes that AI in manufacturing sites (such as vision inspection, digital twin, robotics, AR maintenance), along with edge-cloud distributed inference, will demand uplink, low latency, and stable QoS. AI traffic is projected to surpass non-AI traffic by 2031, and edge-bound AI traffic is expected to grow rapidly. Manufacturers should establish a private 5G/5G-A roadmap considering 5G-A-based uplink enhancement, slicing, and MEC integration, and co-design AI workloads and networks to eliminate bottlenecks. The priorities are (1) defining uplink/latency KPIs by process, (2) localized processing of raw data through MEC proximity deployment, (3) introducing SLA-based network slicing, and (4) optimizing the distribution of inference across cloud, edge, and on-device. Although detailed implementation metrics are scarce due to sponsored research, the strategic direction and implications of traffic shifts are significant for manufacturing AX.

When targeting the Japanese market, prioritize AI, electrification, and eco-friendliness. Structure access to Japanese materials/components through JV, LTA, and joint development, and simultaneously improve lead time and quality through data collaboration, including standardized data schemas, security governance, and federated learning. To build Japanese consumer trust, establish JIS/ISO compliance, digital traceability linked to after-sales service, and closed-loop quality analysis. In the EV/hydrogen value chain, integrate cell/module/pack level SPC and lifetime prediction AI. Additionally, by linking LCA/PCF data sharing and dynamic supply planning (multi-sourcing, derivative BOM) based on demand forecasting AI with the Japanese partner network, procurement competitiveness and brand trust can be enhanced.

The MB.OS-based SDV transition presupposes hardware-software separation, therefore it must internalize standardized interfaces, ECU/domain controller flashing and OTA preparation (remote calibration after EOL), and common diagnostic/test standardization in the production environment. While the expansion of dedicated EV platforms for C, E, and S-Class, and a common OS for ICE and EV, increase variant complexity, this can be addressed with modular tools and jigs, configurable MES, common test sequences, and product/process digital twins, and efficiency can be improved with closed-loop quality through the linkage of vehicle telemetry and factory quality data. Additionally, to leverage the plug-and-play ecosystem with Google, NVIDIA, and Microsoft, API-first integration, SBOM management, UNECE R155/156 compliance, and a software PPAP system must be established as core implementation tasks.

Mercedes-Benz is accelerating its transition to SDVs through dedicated EV platforms for C/E/S-Class vehicles and an integrated software strategy based on MB.OS. From a production perspective, this is an opportunity to manage the increasing number of variants through modularization and standardization, and to establish a common diagnostic/testing system between ICE and EV. Plug-and-play collaboration with Google, NVIDIA, and Microsoft increases the importance of software quality governance, including SBOM, cybersecurity, and software PPAP. Battery cooperation with LG Energy Solution has significant implications for supply chain risk mitigation, requiring production flexibility to accommodate various cell/pack specifications and chemical systems. For manufacturing sites, practical application points include introducing KPIs such as EOL flashing FTT and software release-production synchronization lead time, and preparing for SDV-friendly processes that incorporate digital twin and OTA.

With the proliferation of Blackwell and full production of RTX PRO servers, 20 AI factories in Europe, and Germany's industrial AI cloud, the manufacturing industry has laid the foundation to simultaneously achieve in-region learning/inference, simulation, and data sovereignty; proactive planning for cluster expansion/upgrade and hybrid (on-prem+DGX Cloud) deployment should be undertaken in the 2025-26 budget. For factory and on-site micro data centers, the choice of low-latency fabric (Spectrum-X Ethernet, InfiniBand, NVLink) tailored for mixed learning/inference workloads determines the TCO. As priority application areas, we recommend generative design, process optimization, robot learning/synthetic data, and on-device RTX-based worker assistance/vision inspection. While leveraging sovereign AI investment opportunities, mitigate risks with multi-vendor, multi-cloud, and highly portable MLOps in preparation for export regulations and supply variability.

This announcement clearly demonstrates 'the expansion of AI infrastructure accessibility' and 'the rise of industry-specific clouds' from a manufacturing AI transformation (AX) perspective. Europe's large-scale AI investments and Germany's industrial AI cloud provide a foundation to accelerate model training, simulation, and robot intelligence PoCs for manufacturing. The widespread availability of Blackwell Ultra in the second half of the year, the mass production of RTX PRO servers, and the expansion of networking fabric options suggest that on-premise/edge cluster design and procurement plans should be accelerated. Furthermore, Sovereign AI and localized LLM optimization provide practical value for on-site applications such as multilingual field assistance and document Q&A. However, considering export regulations and geopolitical risks, managing both risk and TCO with a multi-vendor, highly portable MLOps, and hybrid (cloud + on-premise/edge) strategy is key.

In an environment of deglobalization and protectionism, Korean manufacturing, on the premise of reducing reliance on China, must redesign its supply chains with dual/multi-sourcing, re/friend-shoring, and regional production cells, and internalize volatility through TCO+risk-weighted sourcing, price-linked and risk premium contracts, strategic inventory/capacity buffers, and event monitoring. Simultaneously, while transitioning to a high-value 'product+service' model, a roadmap for piloting and scaling AI in areas such as vision inspection, production schedule optimization, demand/material planning, and energy optimization must be supported by data governance, MLOps, OT/IT integration, and re-education for engineer AI literacy. The core is to fundamentally restructure the operating model by establishing an 18-24 month digital and S&OP redesign roadmap, aligned with government incentives, targeting 2026.

The article points out that deglobalization, structural instability, and AI acceleration will unfold simultaneously starting from 2026, emphasizing the need for a strategic shift in Korean manufacturing. From a manufacturing perspective, key directions include supply chain diversification and risk internalization, high value-addition through product-service convergence, and a shift to an AI-centric operational model. In particular, process-specific AI application for quality, scheduling, and demand/energy optimization, along with proactively building a data and MLOps foundation, and the retraining of on-site personnel and securing global talent must be pursued concurrently. The establishment of an 18-24 month implementation roadmap, linked with government policies and incentives, is proposed as a practical response measure.

Tesla's case of abandoning Dojo and switching to Samsung 3nm-based AI5/AI6 and NVIDIA/AMD partnerships presents a practical model that standardizes training-inference into a single architecture, thereby reducing engineering complexity and maintenance costs. Manufacturers must re-evaluate their build vs. buy strategies and ensure rollout/rollback and operational consistency through low-power edge-centric hybrid deployment (cloud training + on-site inference) and SW/HW co-optimization (compiler, runtime, toolchain alignment). Additionally, compare vendors using integrated KPIs such as $/training token, $/inference, performance/watt, TPS, and labor costs, and concurrently implement multi-sourcing to prepare for 3nm yield and supply volatility, along with addressing power and heat issues through inter-generational compatibility and MoE/quantization.

Practical implications from a manufacturing AX perspective: 1) Reduce maintenance and deployment burden by standardizing the training-inference stack, 2) Re-evaluate the AI infrastructure 'build vs. buy' based on TCO, 3) Prepare for mass deployment of low-power edge AI (considering power, heat, and form factor), 4) Manage yield and supply risks when introducing 3nm (multisourcing, compatibility roadmap), 5) Compare vendors using common KPIs (e.g., $/inference, performance/watt), 6) Enhance agility through SW/HW co-optimization and hybrid deployment (cloud training + on-site inference).

Salesforce Agentforce for Manufacturing is a platform that connects production data with sales and service to support real-time decision-making, inventory intelligence (ATP), predictive maintenance, and field service automation. In preparation for tariff-driven cost and price pressures and policy uncertainties, lower unit costs through productivity AI for OEE improvement, process automation, and energy optimization, and simultaneously implement scenario-based supply chain localization/diversification (digital twin verification, origin tracing/compliance) and gated CAPEX and policy KPI monitoring.

Furthermore, practical implications include responding to US localization and policy requirements, strengthening the sub-tier component ecosystem, and aiming for stable ramp-up quality and on-time delivery through digital commissioning and inspection automation.

As nuclear power governance reform directly impacts industrial electricity rates and supply stability, smart factory and AI adoption/expansion plans must be redesigned to reflect CAPEX/OPEX sensitivity to scenarios of 10-20% rate increases and supply fluctuations, and incorporate a long-term power procurement portfolio (PPA/VPPA, long-term fixed rates, on-site solar + ESS/CHP). With the transfer of energy policy functions, electricity price increases and electricity supply uncertainty may intensify, which is expected to impact the investment and operating costs of AI and smart factories in the manufacturing sector. Manufacturers should mitigate risks by conducting power cost sensitivity analysis, establishing a long-term power procurement portfolio (PPA/on-site generation), achieving load flexibility and peak reduction through AI-based EMS, and reviewing backup power sources/ESS. Furthermore, it is practically effective to realign strategies for Scope 2 emissions due to changes in the power mix, RE100, and CBAM compliance, and to continuously monitor policy signals such as electricity tariffs, nuclear power plant schedules, and DR incentives.

If energy functions are separated into a newly established ministry, electricity tariffs, grid capacity, and permits/approvals would operate independently from industrial policy, which could increase cost and supply risks for manufacturing operations as AI and smart factories (including data centers/edge) expand. Therefore, integrated energy-industry governance is crucial. Manufacturing companies should participate in the design of rate plans, PPAs, and DR through consultation channels with the Ministry of Industry, the newly established ministry, and power companies. They should also concurrently establish electricity scenarios that reflect RE100, peak demand, and price fluctuations; ESS and self-generation portfolios; and an energy data and AI-based demand forecasting system, to prepare for policy volatility.

Ulsan City's designation of an AI Innovation Center, along with its dedicated organization, committee, enterprise-wide training, and incentive system, serves as a practical model for manufacturing AI Transformation (AX) governance. This model can clarify the responsibility for on-site application by establishing a cross-functional CoE (Center of Excellence) selected from quality, production, equipment, procurement, and IT; strengthening capabilities based on job-specific curricula; and operating a committee that encompasses internal review, ethics, and data. Manufacturers should, in the short term, focus on pilot projects (including predictive maintenance, logistics optimization, and safety/environmental monitoring) which incorporate learning hours and reward systems, as well as on workforce training. In the mid-to-long term, a strategy that leverages Ulsan City's AI committee, subsidies, and regulatory sandbox to expand inter-factory standardization and the data linkage between supply chains and urban infrastructure is effective.

The core of the article is the strategic perspective that AI should be transformed not into a single-objective optimization tool, but into a 'team partner' that understands humans and context. In manufacturing, multi-objective optimization, including safety, quality, energy, and worker experience, must be reflected in the system design phase, external effects pre-verified with digital twins, and human approval/suspension authority and explainability built-in by default. Practical implications include strengthening exception handling and intent recognition with on-site worker copilots and context-aware UIs, and aligning AI governance and trustworthiness metrics with 'team performance' by including them in operational KPIs.
